Introduction
Purpose
This report provides a comprehensive overview of the current humanitarian situation, focusing on both operational environment
factors and sectoral analysis. It aims to support informed decision-making for humanitarian partners and stakeholders.
About GANNET
GANNET is a Generative AI Initiative from Data Friendly Space, offering AI-powered tools built to make knowledge and real-time
information accessible to our community in a resource-efficient and ethical way. It provides tools such as the GANNET Virtual Assistant
and GANNET SituationHub to help humanitarian actors access, analyze, and utilize critical information for their operations.
GANNET SituationHub
The GANNET SituationHub is a generative AI-powered digital public good designed to identify and analyze humanitarian data from
thousands of trusted sources in real time. By transforming complex data into clear insights, it enables faster, more effective response
strategies and improved coordination among stakeholders.
GANNET Virtual Assistant
The GANNET Virtual Assistant is a verified chatbot designed for humanitarian quick decision-making. Built on cutting-edge Generative
AI and trained with humanitarian expert-reviewed data, it provides real-time information. The GANNET Virtual Assistant facilitates rapid
data access and synthesis for crisis response.
Methodology Overview
The Situation Hub's analytical process uses a structured Human-in-the-Loop workflow that combines automated analysis retrieval
through GANNET Virtual Assistant, DFS's AI-powered humanitarian assistant, with expert review by the DFS analysis team. GANNET
automates the extraction and generation of crisis-related insights based on structured prompts, which are aligned with a tailored
analytical framework. These outputs are then reviewed, validated, and refined by analysts to ensure accuracy, contextual relevance,
and operational utility. The workflow is organized into four key phases: planning and setup, ongoing operations, quality assurance, and
system evolution, ensuring a balance between speed and analytical rigor.
Planning, Setup & Quality Control
In the planning phase, analysts develop a contextualized analytical framework, largely based on the Joint Intersectoral Analysis
Framework (JIAF), and convert it into structured prompts for AI-assisted data extraction. These prompts are refined over time to
improve output quality. Data sources are vetted for reliability, and translations are reviewed for accuracy. Using a centralized Content
Management System (CMS), analysts review and curate AI-generated outputs, validate them against external sources, and make
refinements to enhance clarity and relevance. The CMS enables collaborative editing and version tracking, ensuring outputs align with
humanitarian needs.
Bias Mitigation
To minimize bias, the team diversifies data sources, cross-verifies information, and continuously evaluates the AI's performance.
Analysts are crucial in identifying and correcting inaccuracies, especially in automated categorization. Future improvements aim to
enhance source credibility checks and sentiment analysis, further strengthening the reliability of the Situation Hub's outputs.
Crisis Overview
The SituationHub landing pages use a standardized yet customizable template to deliver consistent, comprehensive crisis overviews.
These pages integrate static content with dynamic data from sources like ACLED and DTM via open APIs, enabling interactive visuals
with real-time updates. Regular reviews and verification with in-country partners ensure accuracy, while manual updates are used for
sources not connected to APIs.
Media Monitoring
The media monitoring process has evolved from a manual, list-based approach to an AI-assisted system integrated into the CMS. This
allows analysts to efficiently review, filter, and contextualize media narratives, while automation helps track emerging trends.
Disclaimer
This tool is provided by SCI oPt CO and DFS and generates content using GANNET, following a multisectoral humanitarian analysis
framework widely adopted and refined for this project. The following content is produced by GANNET under the guidance of
humanitarian analysts, and every output is carefully reviewed and refined by experts to ensure it reflects humanitarian principles,
contextual understanding, and data accuracy before being shared. Please be aware that the information may still include gaps or
inaccuracies and is intended for informational purposes only. It does not constitute official advice or necessarily reflect the official
views or messaging of SCI or DFS.
4
Overview
The following overview has been generated using the information available up to May 25, 2026. It provides a synthesized summary and
key insights into the crisis based on the most recent data accessible at that time.
Summary
The Occupied Palestinian Territory is facing a severe humanitarian crisis, with both Gaza and the West Bank experiencing
unprecedented levels of violence and deprivation. In Gaza, following the collapse of a brief pause in hostilities in March 2025, Israeli
forces resumed intensive bombardment while maintaining a complete blockade that has now entered its eighth week, preventing all
humanitarian aid and commercial supplies from entering. This has left Gaza's 2.2 million residents facing starvation as food stocks
deplete, bakeries cease operations due to lack of flour and fuel, and nearly 90% of the population remains displaced with critical
infrastructure almost entirely destroyed. A new U.S.-backed peace plan led to a pause in hostilities agreement coming into effect on 10
October 2025, raising cautious hopes for increased humanitarian access and a potential reduction in hostilities after months of
relentless violence. However, despite the pause in hostilities, sporadic violations, limited aid entry, and continued displacement
highlight the fragility of the pause in hostilities and the deep humanitarian strain that persists across Gaza.
Meanwhile, the West Bank has seen a dramatic escalation in military operations, particularly since the launch of "Operation Iron Wall"
in January 2025, which has resulted in mass displacement of Palestinians and widespread destruction across refugee camps. Israeli
forces have displaced approximately 40,000 people, with officials stating that residents will not be allowed to return for at least a year,
while expanded checkpoints and military presence severely restrict movement between Palestinian cities. 
International organisations and UN bodies have expressed increasing concern about systematic violations of international
humanitarian law in the West Bank, including accelerating settlement expansion, land reclassification and forced displacement that
multiple observers warn are driving de facto annexation dynamics. These concerns have intensified following the International Court of
Justice's 2024 advisory opinion on the illegality of the prolonged occupation and the Israeli authorities' security cabinet decisions of 8
February 2026 expanding civil and administrative control over West Bank land and governance.
Key Insights
1. Gaza has entered famine conditions, with projections suggesting the crisis could spread to central and southern areas by
September, affecting up to 640,000 people. Between May and September 2025, 470,000 people (22% of the population) were in
Catastrophe/IPC Phase 5, over 1 million in Emergency/Phase 4, and the rest in Crisis/Phase 3—meaning the entire population faces
severe food insecurity. By the end of September, famine conditions were expected to extend beyond Gaza City into Deir al-Balah and
Khan Yunis. Humanitarian aid remains far below needs due to restrictions on access and delivery, driving acute malnutrition, especially
among children, while health and nutrition services collapse under the strain.
2. Over the 2025 April–March period, ~71,000 children under five are projected to suffer from acute malnutrition, with ~14,100 of these
being severe cases. Similarly, nearly 17,000 pregnant and breastfeeding women are expected to require treatment for acute
malnutrition. The rapid rise of malnutrition in children, especially in Gaza City is a red flag: it both signals and drives higher mortality
risks.
3. In the West Bank, "Operation Iron Wall" launched in January 2025 has led to the largest forced displacement since 1967, with
approximately 40,000 Palestinians displaced from refugee camps including Jenin, Tulkarem, Nur Shams, and El Far\'a, with Israeli
officials stating residents cannot return for at least a year.
4. Israeli forces deployed tanks in the northern West Bank in February 2025 for the first time in 20 years, while establishing at least 20
new iron gates and numerous checkpoints throughout the territory, severely restricting movement between Palestinian cities and
villages.
5. According to World Bank assessments (February 2025), reconstruction and recovery needs in Gaza require approximately US$53
billion, with housing accounting for 53% of damages, while commerce and industry represent 20%, and critical infrastructure including
health, water, and transport comprising 15%.
6. The collapse of local food systems is near total , 98% of cropland in Gaza is reported as destroyed or inaccessible, and fishing has
been banned or heavily restricted. Because of wartime blockades and displacement, the normal supply chains for food, fuel, water,
and medical supplies have been disrupted or severed. In Gaza City and Khan Yunis, wheat flour prices have reportedly jumped ~3,000%
compared to earlier months. Many households are resorting to extreme coping strategies: selling clothes, foraging or collecting trash,
or going whole days without food.
5
Operational Environment
Economy
Current Economic Situation
General
Since late April 2026, official indicators show a sharp national economic contraction — GDP contracted by around 24%, with the Gaza
economy collapsing by about 84% and the West Bank declining roughly 13% — and unemployment reaching roughly 46% overall (78%
in Gaza; 28% in the West Bank), leaving more than 650,000 people jobless [19]. Restrictions on movement and the blockade are
estimated to cost the Palestinian economy about US$3.4 billion per year (around 20% of GDP), and together with a weak industrial
base (industry's share under 10% of GDP) and extensive damage to Gaza's industrial facilities, have driven large-scale loss of
production, massive job destruction, and deepened poverty and market disruption [1]. The Palestinian Authority faces a fiscal crisis
with public debt exceeding US$14 billion amid prolonged withheld tax/clearance revenues [36][4][43][66][31]. Humanitarian access
and commercial flows remain severely constrained: agreed daily entries of 600 trucks are not being met, with actual average entries
well below target (about 227–234 trucks per day) and fuel deliveries far below requirements, further exacerbating service failures and
shortages [34][7].
Conflict Economic Landscape
Gaza
The joint EU–UN–World Bank Rapid Damage and Needs Assessment estimates Gaza's recovery and reconstruction needs at US$71.4
billion over the next decade, including US$26.3 billion required in the first 18 months [4][31][36][43][66]. The Ministry of Public Works
and Housing signed a five-year Memorandum of Understanding with UNOPS on 27 April 2026 to support early recovery and planned
reconstruction — covering road networks, public buildings, transitional shelter, supply-chain facilitation, debris recycling, GIS damage
quantification and capacity building — and to mobilize resources from international donors [12][20]. Key damage figures include:
- Housing and displacement: more than 371,000 housing units were destroyed or damaged and over 60% of Gaza's population
remain homeless or displaced [4][36].
- Health and WASH: over 50% of hospitals are non-functional; approximately 90% of water and sanitation infrastructure has been
destroyed or damaged; and over 18,500 patients require evacuation amid critical shortages of medicines and medical supplies
[4][43][7].
- Agriculture: more than 86% of Gaza's agricultural land was affected, with near-total collapse of water and livestock systems;
comprehensive agricultural reconstruction costs are estimated at over US$4.2 billion, while available funding covers less than
10% of urgent needs [13].
- Industry and businesses: roughly 75% of Gaza's industrial facilities and about 80% of small businesses were destroyed or
damaged, with over 200,000 jobs lost in Gaza alone [1][11].
- Rubble clearance: UNDP estimates that 8,000 bodies remain under rubble and less than 1% of rubble has been removed to date,
with clearance projected to take up to seven years at the current pace; UNDP has removed roughly 287,000 tonnes so far [4][21].
Labour force participation in Gaza collapsed from approximately 55% pre-war to around 32% by end-2024 and further to about 25% by
late 2025, reflecting massive job losses and market exit [8][1]. Trade unions and ministries report some 550,000 workers without
income and a surge in informal and high-risk coping activities such as debris work, compounded by very low social-protection
coverage [30][8][14]. UN experts and officials caution that reconstruction finance and large-scale investment will require credible
governance, human-rights safeguards, and political conditions — including accountability over occupation-related constraints —
before major donors commit funds [36].
Stability of Currency and Bank System
General
The Palestinian Authority faces a severe fiscal crisis driven by the prolonged withholding of tax and clearance revenues, with public
debt rising above US$14 billion [31][43][73][66][35]. Prime Minister Mohammad Mustafa publicly urged the EU and other international
partners to press for the release of these funds during meetings on 28–29 April 2026 [73][66][35]. Palestinian officials continue to use
donor-coordination fora, including the AHLC and upcoming donor meetings, to call for increased political and financial support to
stabilize institutions and implement UNSC Resolution 2803 [66][73]. Public-sector employees have experienced partial and delayed
salary payments, with payroll disbursements reported at about 50–70% in some months, increasing household indebtedness and
reliance on loan deductions from wages [10]. As a liquidity mitigation measure, the Ministry of Social Development — in cooperation
with the Monetary and Banking Authority — has expanded electronic payment (e-payment) systems to deliver direct cash support to
needy families and women [61].
Employment
General
Unemployment figures reported around World Labour Day (end-April/May 2026) vary by source. Official PCBS data indicate Gaza
unemployment at approximately 68%, with youth (ages 15–29) NEET rates around 74%, and West Bank unemployment at 28% in Q4
2025 (up from 13% pre-war), reflecting a loss of about 132,000 jobs in the West Bank alone [1]. Trade unions report higher figures, citing
Gaza unemployment as high as 85% and West Bank unemployment at approximately 38%, with roughly 550,000 workers unemployed
6
across the occupied Palestinian territory [27][30]. The Gaza economy contracted by roughly 83–84% during 2024–25, with industrial
damage exceeding 90–95%, driving widespread dependence on humanitarian assistance [11][4][16].
In terms of labour quality and informality, approximately 43% of private-sector wage workers in the West Bank work without formal
contracts, roughly 34% of employees earn below the statutory minimum wage (with higher shares among women and in Gaza), and
average daily wages stand at approximately 112 shekels in the West Bank and 68 shekels in Gaza [10][1]. Social security coverage and
unemployment insurance have remained suspended since 2016 [10][1]. Gender disparities are pronounced: women's unemployment
and precarious work are markedly higher — cited at around 60% in some statements — with high shares of women working without
contracts and rising numbers of female-headed households in Gaza since the onset of the war [19][18].
Trade unions report tens of workers killed and tens of thousands arrested since October 2023, the suspension of work permits for many
formerly employed inside Israel, hazardous informal coping strategies (including risky border crossings), and widespread denial of
access to prior workplaces [38][27]. Labour law enforcement remains weak, with very low inspector numbers relative to international
standards, large legal gaps on harassment and disability quotas, and calls for urgent adoption and enforcement of amended labour
legislation [1]. Public salaries have been paid partially or late in many months (50–70% value reported), and clearance revenue
withholding combined with blockade-related restrictions constrain both public hiring and reconstruction-linked job creation [10][20].
Politics
Governance Structures and Rule of Law
General
The Palestinian leadership is advancing a comprehensive reform programme to develop state institutions, strengthen governance
and transparency, unify social protection, bolster the rule of law and digital transformation, and reform security and justice sectors
while preparing a constitution, a political parties law and a general elections law [191]. The programme explicitly includes Fatah youth
elections, local council elections, the Palestinian National Council elections scheduled for November, and preparatory work for
presidential and legislative elections to be held when conditions permit [191]. Prime Minister Mohammad Mustafa informed the EU
Special Envoy that over 70% of the government's reform agenda has been completed, citing progress on governance, rule of law,
transparency, improved public services and the successful conduct of recent local elections [192][194]. Palestinian and international
interlocutors discussed accelerating implementation of UN Security Council Resolution 2803 to consolidate the ceasefire, facilitate
humanitarian access, advance recovery and reconstruction, and expedite transitional arrangements toward reunification of Gaza and
the West Bank under a single Palestinian authority [194][191]. On 29 April 2026, Prime Minister Mustafa also briefed the EU Special
Representative that escalations in the West Bank, systematic settler terrorism, ongoing settlement expansion and the withholding of
Palestinian tax revenues for nearly a year are undermining Palestinian institutions and exacerbating humanitarian needs [194].
Gaza
UN experts warned that reconstruction in Gaza cannot succeed without ending the occupation and ensuring Palestinian-led,
rights-based rebuilding, and cited the Gaza Rapid Damage and Needs Assessment with the following estimates:
- More than 371,000 housing units destroyed or damaged [180]
- 1.9 million people displaced [180]
- Over 60% of the population remaining homeless [180]
- Reconstruction needs estimated at more than $71 billion [180]
Gaza trade unions and worker groups have called for stronger enforcement of the Palestinian Labour Code, improved oversight of
workplaces, operationalization of the Ministry of Labour and trade unions, protection of women workers from discrimination, and
prioritization of reconstructing industrial areas to restore livelihoods [179]. Reporters Without Borders documented a global
deterioration in press freedom and reported that since 7 October 2023 more than 220 journalists have been killed in Gaza, at least 70 of
them while performing their duties, noting a significant decline in legal protections for journalists [119]. Israeli legislation enacted since
2024 enables prohibition of foreign media broadcasts deemed to threaten state security, constraining media freedom in the occupied
territories, and access for independent foreign journalists to Gaza has remained restricted since October 2023 [129][115][119]. The
International Criminal Court prosecutor reported sustained external pressure and threats following warrants related to the conflict,
including sanctions from third states that his office said aimed to undermine the enforcement of international law [160].
Political Freedoms and Recent Laws
West Bank
Israeli trends in the West Bank — settlement expansion, increased settler attacks, the creation of Israeli civilian administrative bodies,
and measures undermining the Palestinian Authority — are described as reconfiguring control from temporary occupation toward
effective permanent sovereignty and closing off constitutional and political prospects for a two-State solution [137][202]. Specific
policy and planning changes include the transfer of planning powers, establishment of Israeli administrative bodies on holy sites,
delays in financial clearing, restrictions on Palestinian workers, and sanctions against Palestinian officials [137]. Key quantitative
impacts include the approval of 34 new settlements in a single resolution at the end of March 2026 and the allocation of 2.7 billion
shekels for infrastructure development outside the Green Line [137]. Settler attacks, a declining security deterrent and a "two-tier"
policing reality have increased threats to Palestinian safety and limited access to basic services including healthcare [137][187].
7
Since October 2025, OCHA reported a rise in limited incursions, targeting of residential areas near lines of contact, and increasing
movement restrictions that functionally erode the ceasefire agreement [106]. 
Gaza
Reports also indicate Israeli consultations about possible transfer of people from Gaza — displacement scenarios rejected
internationally — and, combined with restrictions on agreed aid entry, these measures heighten risks to political rights and civic space
in Gaza [108][197].
A study by Salama found that a recently adopted law on the execution of prisoners reclassifies militant acts as criminal offenses and
has shifted the centre of Palestinian confrontation from street mobilization toward legal and diplomatic channels, reducing the street's
capacity to mobilize [109]. The study describes an integrated "triple blockade": Israeli arrest campaigns exceeding 23,000 arrests since
October 2023 targeting field actors and youth cadres; the replacement of prisoners' allowances with a social-protection framework;
and the institutionalisation of prisoners' issues within legal and administrative ceilings — measures that weaken symbolic and material
community links and constrain popular action [109].
Influence of Non-State Actors and Political Factions
Gaza
Hamas has publicly rejected proposals for immediate disarmament, tying any surrender of arms to guarantees of an independent
Palestinian state and insisting arms arrangements be part of a broader political framework rather than a unilateral Israeli demand
[209][204][210]. Palestinian factions — including Hamas, Palestinian Islamic Jihad and the Popular Front — have presented a unified
rejection of disarmament preconditions, insisting Israel first implement ceasefire commitments, withdraw from expanded areas and
allow agreed humanitarian flows before weapons are addressed [211][210][209]. Mediators, notably Nikolai Mladenov, circulated a
phased roadmap that conditions reconstruction, aid and crossings on staged handovers of weapons; Palestinian leaders and analysts
have described this approach as tantamount to political surrender, and it has been rejected by Israeli and US interlocutors, increasing
the risk of renewed hostilities [210][211][108].
Israeli control of territory and infrastructure via expanding "yellow" and "orange" lines is being used to reconfigure governance and limit
civilian movement, access to services and humanitarian operations, effectively influencing post-conflict arrangements on the ground
[114][106][125]. Key humanitarian-access figures illustrate this control:
- As of March 2026, Israeli forces controlled or forbade access to about 58% of the Gaza Strip, restricting population and
humanitarian movement [125]
- The WASH cluster assessed that 43% of WASH facilities were inaccessible to the population and humanitarians as of March 2026
[125]
- An additional 10% of WASH facilities were accessible only with Israeli permission [125]
Palestinian commentators and diaspora leaders emphasize that national unity is viewed as essential to restore political leverage and
effective international advocacy [136]. Together, armed factions' leverage on negotiations, unified factional resistance to disarmament
without political guarantees, and Israel's territorial-control practices are actively reshaping governance, security arrangements and
negotiation leverage in Gaza and the wider Palestinian political landscape [209][211][114][125].
Public Trust in State Institutions
West Bank
Israeli administrative measures in the West Bank — including the establishment of Israeli administrative bodies on holy sites, delays in
financial-clearing transfers, sanctions against Palestinian officials, and persistent settler attacks — have reduced the Palestinian
Authority's functionality and political legitimacy [137]. Deepening Palestinian political division and weakened organizational structures
have shifted action away from street mobilization toward diplomatic and legal channels, reducing public confidence in grassroots
capacity to influence outcomes [109][136]. Legal moves that criminalize militant acts, such as measures described around the law on
execution of prisoners, have further constrained public protest and undermined confidence in traditional forms of resistance [109].
In response to these dynamics, President Mahmoud Abbas and Fatah have publicly called for renewal of legitimacy through local
elections, a transitional constitution and National Assembly elections, framing the eighth Fatah conference — convened as a hinge
moment in mid-May 2026 — as an effort to rebuild trust in institutions and political participation [138][163][136]. Palestinian
communities in Europe have played an organized pressure role since October 7, helping shift the issue onto European public agendas
and contributing to some recognition moves [136][172]. Collectively, these dynamics point to an erosion of trust in Palestinian
institutions, while political actors prioritise elections, institutional reform and diplomatic engagement to restore legitimacy
[137][138][109].
Role of Marginalized Groups
Gaza
Fatah's eighth general conference is being presented by leaders as an opportunity to integrate diaspora, youth and women into the
national project alongside broader institutional renewal [136][138][163]. Palestinian communities in the diaspora, particularly in Europe,
have played an organized advocacy role since October 2023, while youth cadres have been disproportionately affected by Israeli
arrest campaigns — with more than 23,000 arrests recorded since October 2023 — constraining youth participation in grassroots
political action [109][136][172].
8
The severe damage to Gaza's agricultural base — with 86% of agricultural lands damaged — has undermined livelihoods and
economic inclusion, driving appeals for a development-oriented reconstruction approach rather than only emergency response
[181][168]. Gaza trade unions have specifically called for protection of women workers from discrimination and prioritization of
reconstructing industrial areas to restore livelihoods, reflecting the gendered dimensions of economic exclusion [179]. Settler attacks, a
declining security deterrent and a "two-tier" policing reality have increased threats to Palestinian safety and limited access to basic
services including healthcare, with marginalized communities bearing a disproportionate burden [137][187]. Observers describe a
redefinition of Palestinian political space since late March 2026: constrained on-the-ground mobilisation, increased reliance on
diplomatic and legal avenues, and weakened institutional capacity to deliver services and represent citizens locally [109][137][163].
Security And Stability
Security Overview
General
Between late April and early May 2026, UN agencies reported persistent hostilities and attacks disrupting civilian life and humanitarian
operations in Gaza and the occupied West Bank [241]. OCHA and UN spokespeople reported that strikes across Gaza continued to hit
residential areas and critical infrastructure; an airstrike on a Gaza City water well killed one NGO worker and injured four, and
operations at the Al Mansoura water-filling point were suspended after two UNICEF-contracted drivers were killed earlier in April,
leaving around 60% of families without adequate clean water [274][241]. WHO reported 149 attacks on the health sector, resulting in
100 deaths and 233 injuries, underscoring severe risks to health workers and facilities [274][241]. Explosive-weapons use remained a
major cause of civilian harm in Gaza in 2025, with AOAV recording Gaza among the most impacted areas globally (approximately
2,530 incidents), leaving persistent explosive hazards for civilians and humanitarian operations [226][246].
Aid-worker mortality remains elevated globally and in the Palestinian territories, with 326 aid workers killed in 2025 and over 560 killed
in Gaza and the West Bank across recent years [241][270]. Three civil-defence workers were also killed in southern Lebanon while
responding to strikes [241]. UNIFIL recorded 81 firing incidents by Israeli forces south of the Blue Line and reported interference with
patrols, highlighting cross-border security risks that complicate regional stabilization and aid delivery [274][241].
Settler violence and expanded Israeli control measures in the West Bank are driving tensions, eroding Palestinian Authority
functionality, hampering humanitarian access, and raising prospects of further violent confrontations [222][241]. UN briefings warned
that continued settler attacks and Israeli military operations in the West Bank are undermining Palestinian Authority institutions [241].
Work permits for more than 200,000 Palestinian workers have been suspended since October 2023, with 74 worker deaths documented
in 2025, approximately 38,000 arrests of workers, and sharply elevated unemployment — estimated between approximately 68%–85%
in Gaza and around 28%–38% in the West Bank in late 2025 — exacerbating protection and livelihoods risks [281][288][282]. Journalists
and media workers have been targeted and detained, including reports of a journalist dying in Megiddo prison in March 2026,
reflecting shrinking space for information and additional protection risks [265]. Reports of Israeli consultations on possible transfer of
people from Gaza to other states signal continued displacement risk [216], and regional officials warned that continued instability
could trigger new irregular migration flows from Lebanon and the wider region, adding further protection and border-security
pressures [278].
Security Forces and Law Enforcement
General
Israeli forces detained and interrogated approximately 140 Palestinians across the West Bank, including Jerusalem, on 27–28 April
2026, with most later released [248]. Since October 2023, arrest campaigns in the West Bank have exceeded 23,000 detentions, carried
out through mass raids and field interrogations that human-rights groups say involve beatings, property destruction, and other
abuses [224][248]. The Palestinian Prisoners' Society reported that at least 1,251 Palestinians abducted from Gaza have been
designated "unlawful combatants" and held in prolonged arbitrary detention, and occupation courts renewed the detention of notable
detainees such as Hossam Abu Safiya in late April 2026 [294]. UN experts, Palestinian organisations, and independent investigators
have documented allegations of systematic torture, medical neglect, starvation, and sexual violence in detention and during raids,
and noted that more than 100 detainees have died in captivity since October 2023, including 32 deaths in 2025 [224][256][294].
Settler violence and Israeli military raids in the West Bank have included home invasions, the conversion of homes into temporary
military posts, attacks on humanitarian infrastructure including a raid on an UNRWA clinic, and a spike in killings and injuries recorded
between 14 March and 16 April 2026 [271][253]. Gaza's local policing capacity has been severely degraded — police forces have been
described as decimated and police/civilian infrastructure repeatedly targeted — undermining prospects for re-establishing a
centralized Palestinian security authority in the Strip [238][230][220]. International ceasefire-monitoring and coordination
mechanisms are being scaled down or restructured, with reports that U.S. personnel linked to a Gaza ceasefire unit may be cut from
around 190 to approximately 40, raising concerns about enforcement and humanitarian coordination on the ground [223][234][250].
Demography
Demographic Overview
Gaza
Israeli mapping and UN reporting indicate Gaza's population has been increasingly confined into a narrow coastal area, with nearly
two million people almost entirely constrained to a small sliver of the Strip as Israel exerts effective control over roughly twothirds of
Gaza's territory [312][333]. UN sources documented orders moving up to 2.1 million people into a 43.3 km² "humanitarian zone,"
producing an estimated density of about 48,500 people per km² in that zone [296]. Displacement is nearuniversal and repeated: joint
9
UN/World Bank assessments and UN reporting estimate roughly 1.9 million people displaced in Gaza, with more than 60% of the
population rendered homeless by destruction of housing and infrastructure [327][313]. Around 90% of Gazans have been forcibly
displaced at least once, while movement monitoring recorded large internal displacements of about 198,000 movements between
14–17 September 2025, rising to nearly 340,000 by 23 September 2025, indicating multiple, repeated relocations for many families
[306][296]. Demographic trends show a sharp deterioration: Gaza's population growth turned negative (1.3%), with birth rates falling
38% in 2024 and a further 13% in 2025, accompanied by large increases in preterm births, low birth weights, and neonatal mortality
[302]. In the West Bank, UN data report approximately 40,000 Palestinians forcibly displaced since January 2025, and demographic
pressure is compounded by an estimated settler population of about 750,000 living in 141 settlements and 224 outposts, including
roughly 250,000 in East Jerusalem [299][308]. Palestinian authorities have digitized social registers covering roughly 350,000 families
(about 90% of the population) to target emergency cash assistance amid repeated displacement and shelter loss [324].
Socio-Cultural
Socio-Cultural Overview
Gaza
The social fabric in Gaza and across the occupied Palestinian territory has been severely strained by mass, repeated displacement
and shrinking living space, with UN reporting that some 90% of Gazans have been forcibly displaced at least once and warning of
collapsed services and extreme psychological pressure on communities [339][353]. Security operations, expansive arrest campaigns
(reported at over 23,000 arrests since October 2023) and recent legislation have shifted political contestation from street-based
popular mobilisation to institutional and legal channels, weakening organised public action and social mobilisation capacity [377].
Recurrent attacks and policies have directly targeted communal and religious spaces, with documented increases in settler incursions
into the Al-Aqsa compound and widespread destruction of mosques and other religious sites, further degrading shared cultural and
spiritual anchors [408][371]. East Jerusalem communities face accelerating demolitions and eviction pressures — for example, 17
homes demolished in Silwan's al-Bustan in 2026 and warnings of thousands more at risk — contributing to demographic engineering
and local social dislocation [365]. Targeting of civic and humanitarian infrastructure, including raids that damaged or closed UNRWA
clinics and other community services, has disrupted neighbourhood-level support networks and trust in public institutions [412].
Large-scale job losses — estimated at roughly 550,000 Palestinian workers — combined with weak social protection have undermined
traditional livelihoods and household stability, with unemployment in Gaza reaching about 68% and 74% of young people out of school
and work according to PCBS (April 2026) [380][356][341]. Amid these pressures, authorities and social actors have turned to digital
mechanisms for social assistance — such as a digital social register covering some 350,000 families used to coordinate emergency
cash support — indicating adaptive changes in how social solidarity and service delivery are organised under crisis conditions [350].
Gender Dynamics
General
UN experts warned that sexual and gender-based violence has become a systematic tool of control and a driver of forced
displacement in the occupied Palestinian territory, reported across detention centres, checkpoints, house raids and interactions with
both Israeli forces and settlers, with investigations and accountability largely absent [373][378]. Palestinian civil society and detainee
monitors also document widespread torture and sexual assaults in detention contexts, further undermining survivors' trust in
authorities and complicating protection and referral pathways [425]. Overcrowding in displacement sites and dwindling resources
have heightened psychological distress and increased risks of gender-based violence for women and children, with humanitarian
actors warning of strained protection conditions and rising vulnerabilities [361]. Gendered safety risks at service points — for example,
women including pregnant women reporting they cannot safely fetch water, with children sent in their stead — further increase
protection vulnerabilities and reduce safe access to humanitarian distributions [364].
Destruction of sexual and reproductive health services and severe WASH constraints have increased reproductive health risks and
dignity harms for women and girls, including shortages of menstrual supplies and unsafe menstrual practices that have raised
infection risks, and have reduced access to maternal care [378][364]. Key socioeconomic indicators reported by the Palestinian
Ministry of Women's Affairs (statement by Minister Mona Al-Khalili, 30 April 2026) include an overall GDP contraction of ~24%, a collapse
of Gaza's economy by ~84%, and a West Bank decline of ~13%, with overall unemployment rising to ~46% (78% in Gaza, 28% in the West
Bank) and more than 650,000 people unemployed [354]. Women's labour conditions are especially precarious: 41.5% of women work
without contracts, 30% on verbal agreements, and only 47.2% receive paid maternity leave, with persistent gender wage gaps noted
[354]. UNDP/ESCWA-cited estimates further report a poverty rate of 74.3%, women's unemployment around 60%, female labour force
participation at 18–19%, and female-headed households in Gaza rising to 15–18% [382].
The Palestinian Ministry of Social Development has scaled digital social-registry and e-payment systems in Gaza (covering some
350,000 families) to deliver emergency cash and target assistance, and national agricultural and recovery programmes explicitly
include components aimed at women's economic empowerment and access to livelihoods [350][383]. UN experts have also
highlighted that reconstruction and housing processes risk reinforcing pre-existing discrimination unless they prioritise widows,
female-headed households and women's equal access to land, housing and participation in decision-making about rebuilding [393].
Impact of Socio-Cultural Drivers on Aid
General
Perceptions of international inaction and "functional silence" by external actors have eroded community trust and are described as
facilitating control over basic goods and aid flows, which undermines acceptance of and confidence in humanitarian interventions
[405][393].
10
The fragile information environment and attacks on journalists constrain accurate two-way communication and accountability,
reducing community trust and complicating outreach and feedback mechanisms for humanitarian actors [395]. The systematic use
of sexual and gender-based violence as a tool of control, combined with widespread stigma and barriers to accessing services,
creates significant protection needs that humanitarian programmes must navigate [378][373]. Entrenched labour precarity —
including women working without contracts, wage gaps, and harassment — reduces women's labour participation and economic
resilience, shaping the profile of need and the design required for effective targeted social protection [354][382]. National and local
programming and reconstruction proposals explicitly prioritise women's empowerment and local partnerships to strengthen
community resilience and livelihoods, indicating programme-level recognition that socio-cultural drivers must be addressed to
improve aid effectiveness [383][385].
Environment
Environmental Conditions
Gaza
Gaza's geographic and climatic vulnerability is compounded by near-total collapse of its water and sanitation infrastructure. UN, EU,
and World Bank assessments indicate roughly 89–90% of WASH infrastructure has been damaged or destroyed, including desalination
plants, boreholes, pipelines, and sewage networks [465][458][483][433]. Bombing in April 2026 further damaged desalination,
distribution, and pumping systems, leaving more than two million people with extremely limited access to safe drinking water and, in
some areas, receiving under 6 litres per person per day [477]. Water quality is critically degraded: over 90% of available water is
non-potable due to salinity and contamination from collapsed sanitation systems and sewage infiltration [477][458].
Gaza's agricultural sector has been similarly devastated. A Palestinian Centre study estimated that over 86% of agricultural land has
been affected, with near-total collapse of water and livestock systems, threatening food security and long-term land rehabilitation
[462]. Fuel and power shortages have further degraded WASH operations and healthcare capacity, while WHO documented reduced
healthcare capacity, ongoing WASH system degradation, and verified attacks on health services during the March–April 2026
escalation, complicating repair and response efforts [478][477]. In the West Bank, damage to pipes and wells and attacks on water
infrastructure have left Palestinian communities with water consumption well below WHO-recommended levels [464].
Environmental Challenges
General
The collapse of water, sanitation, and waste management systems has generated severe and compounding public health risks.
Solid-waste management has collapsed, with UNDP reporting approximately 900,000 tonnes of accumulated waste from October
2023 through December 2025, linked to large rodent and insect outbreaks that heighten epidemic risk [456][463]. Over 17,000 rodent-
and parasitic-related injuries among displaced people have been reported since the start of the year, alongside roughly 111,500
external-parasite cases in the same period [467][451][435]. Health actors have warned of imminent outbreaks of cholera,
leptospirosis, scabies, and other vector- and waterborne diseases, and diarrhoeal, skin, and respiratory infections have been rising
among populations served by medical actors in 2025–2026 [467][478][456][477][458].
Shelter shortages and severe overcrowding in tents — with only approximately 2,000 housing units delivered, representing less than 1%
of assessed need — have amplified sanitary risks and disease transmission pathways [451][460]. Restricted fuel and the denial or
delay of key repair materials and pesticides have impeded restoration of WASH services and pest control, undermining public-health
responses and infrastructure rehabilitation [465][456]. MSF reported that between May and November 2025, one in five of its water
distributions ran dry while people were still waiting, reflecting severe shortages and access constraints [471][465][458]. Calls for urgent
WHO technical support, emergency pest-control measures, and sustained WASH, waste management, and epidemiological
interventions have been issued by WHO and other agencies [456][467][463][478].
Environmental Risks and Mitigation
Gaza
Gaza faces a layered set of environmental hazards that severely constrain recovery. Unexploded ordnance is widespread, estimated
at over 70,000 items by the UN Mine Action Service, and has caused casualties while impeding recovery and rubble-clearance
operations; UNMAS documented more than 1,200 people killed or injured by explosive ordnance in Gaza since October 2023
[455][440][447]. Gaza's total estimated rubble stands at approximately 61 million tonnes, and clearance could take up to seven years
under accelerated, unimpeded access assumptions; to date, UNDP has cleared about 287,000 tonnes [446][447]. Reconstruction
needs are estimated at approximately USD 71.4 billion over the next decade, with about USD 26.3 billion required in the first 18 months,
according to joint EU–UN–World Bank assessments [447][444]. One-third of requests to bring critical WASH supplies — including
desalination units, pumps, chlorine, generators, spare parts, and related items — into Gaza were rejected or left unanswered,
constraining repairs and maintenance [433].
Mitigation efforts are ongoing but severely constrained. UNDP's rubble-crushing and reuse programme rehabilitates roads and creates
shelter and community spaces, reducing demand for imported construction materials, but depends on heavy machinery, fuel, and
explosive-ordnance clearance coordinated with UNMAS [446][447]. MSF reported producing and distributing over 4.7 million litres of
water daily as of January 2026 [458]. Humanitarian and local actors — including the Gaza Centre for Human Rights, WHO, and MSF —
have repeatedly called for sustained opening of crossings and immediate entry of pesticides, rodent-control agents, heavy
equipment, generators, and fuel to address waste, pest infestations, and WASH repairs; while COGAT permitted nearly 1,000 rat traps
and approximately 10 tonnes of pesticides, major denials and delays persist [456][433][435]. Without sustained, unimpeded access to
11
heavy equipment, fuel, WASH parts, and systematic explosive-ordnance clearance, recovery, waste management, and
epidemic-prevention measures will remain severely constrained.
Conflict
Overview of the Ongoing Armed Conflict
Gaza
Since the ceasefire that entered into force in October 2025, Gaza has seen persistent violations and localized escalations, including
renewed strikes, artillery and naval fire in late April 2026 [541][604]. Heavy strikes caused at least 40 reported deaths between 20–27
April 2026, and thousands of ceasefire breaches have been recorded overall — more than 2,400 violations through mid-April and
approximately 377 incidents in April alone [604][541][562]. Cumulative casualty figures reported by Gaza health authorities remain
very high: roughly 72,600 killed and approximately 172,400 wounded since 7 October 2023, with roughly 800–830 killed and some
2,300–2,345 wounded recorded since the ceasefire as of late April 2026 [572][562].
Active hostilities and strikes are concentrated across the Gaza Strip — including Rafah, Khan Younis, Deir al-Balah, Gaza City, Jabalia
and surrounding eastern neighbourhoods — while violence, raids and escalating settler attacks continue across the occupied West
Bank and East Jerusalem [562][606][575][585]. Operations have involved airstrikes, heavy artillery, demolition of houses, naval
shelling and ground incursions [581][606]. Israeli forces have also expanded their presence in Gaza beyond previously agreed
ceasefire boundaries, with reports of an "orange line" extending past the "yellow line" [561]. Policy and territorial moves — including
expanding control lines and approvals for new settlements reported in March–April 2026 — are deepening spatial control and
complicating protection and access [604][539][557].
Critical civilian infrastructure, notably hospitals and other health facilities, has been extensively damaged or rendered
non-operational, with the health system described as collapsed in places. Health system capacity and WASH services have been
further degraded by fuel shortages and damage to facilities, as verified by WHO and other monitors [581][606][526]. UN/EU/World
Bank estimates put reconstruction needs at approximately $71.4 billion over a decade, with an initial $26.3 billion required for the first 18
months [572][521][549]. Humanitarian access remains severely constrained by blockade and entry restrictions on goods (including
limits on items labelled "dual use"), routine impediments to rescue and ambulance access, and ongoing security risks for aid delivery
[571][572][538]. WHO and other agencies have verified multiple attacks on health services during the escalation, underscoring
persistent protection risks for civilians and humanitarian responders [526].
Conflict Stakeholders
General
Israeli state forces have continued offensive and occupation actions in Gaza and the West Bank. Médecins Sans Frontières reported
repeated destruction and denial of water, sanitation and hygiene infrastructure in Gaza, attacks on clearly marked MSF water trucks,
regular cuts to Mekorot supply lines, and reduced functionality of pre-October networks, with a UAE-funded pipeline to southern Gaza
operating since August 2025 [611].
Gaza
Hamas and other Gaza-based armed factions reject immediate disarmament demands. Mediators' proposals in Cairo reportedly
required all armed groups in Gaza to surrender heavy weapons and maps of tunnel networks within 90 days — a proposal the factions
opposed as risking a security vacuum [561][516]. Internal factional divisions persist, with some groups demanding comprehensive,
phased arrangements and the handing-over of arms to an official Palestinian entity such as police, while others resist, representing a
key spoiler risk to transition and stabilization efforts [516]. Palestinian negotiators have insisted that any security arrangements must
be linked to statehood and political guarantees, and a joint Palestinian proposal on Gaza's transition was reported to have been
rejected by the United States and Israel [561].
External actors remain active but constrained. The US-led CMCC/ISF architecture has struggled to translate plans into on-the-ground
security commitments, with only a handful of troop pledges, no country committing active deployment into Gaza, and declining
diplomatic momentum around the CMCC [538]. The Palestinian Authority is engaging international partners to implement UNSC
Resolution 2803 and advance transitional arrangements for Gaza and West Bank reunification, seeking donor and diplomatic support
for recovery and governance [597].
Humanitarian institutions report severe service collapse in Gaza: the UN indicated no hospital in Gaza was fully operational and around
18,000 people awaited authorization for medical evacuation — conditions that drive political pressure and constrain reintegration
plans [593][561].
Peace Negotiations
Gaza
The Gaza ceasefire that began in October 2025 remains fragile, with Israeli strikes and reported violations continuing. Diplomatic and
stabilisation mechanisms are in flux: diplomats and Reuters sources report the United States plans to scale down or fold the
Civil-Military Coordination Centre (CMCC) into a US-led International Stabilisation Force (ISF), while the Board of Peace denied the
CMCC was closing and defended its role [494][498][503]. The proposed ISF transition has seen limited international troop pledges and
has not been deployed as intended, raising questions about its authority to enforce the ceasefire and guarantee aid delivery on the
ground [504][494].
12
Efforts to advance to a negotiated phase II are stalled by the "weapon file" (disarmament) and divergent party positions. Hamas has
rejected proposals to disarm without explicit guarantees of Palestinian statehood, tying any security-sector concessions to political
recognition and complicating post-war arrangements [618][502]. Cairo mediations continue, but progress is limited by substantive
disagreements and by Israeli measures that Palestinian leaders say are undermining institutions and constraining humanitarian
access [516][597][623].
On the humanitarian side, only 296 of 683 health service points are functioning, 104 patients were medically evacuated via Rafah
between 20–26 April, and studies report 96% of Gaza cropland damaged or inaccessible [493][598].
Conflict Outlook
Gaza
Between 27 April and 4 May 2026 the situation remains volatile. The October 2025 ceasefire is fragile, with continued Israeli violations
and renewed raids in late April raising the risk of a return to wider ground operations [541][622]. Efforts to advance to a negotiated
phase II are stalled by the "weapon file" (disarmament) and divergent party positions — Palestinian factions gave conditional
responses to a 15-point proposal while Israel has not committed — creating a real danger that political failure will precipitate further
escalation [516].
External drivers increase spillover risk: sustained US military support and large arms shipments to Israel, together with a deadlocked
US–Iran negotiating environment, amplify the chance of multi-front dynamics and broader regional escalation [497][506]. Diplomatic
activity continues through Cairo mediations, UN representation, and EU-Palestine engagements, but progress remains limited.
Meetings of Prime Minister Mustafa on 28–29 April highlighted concerns about Israeli measures undermining Palestinian institutions
and constraining humanitarian access [516][597][623].
Humanitarian consequences are likely to persist and worsen: most Gaza hospitals are not fully operational, thousands await medical
evacuation, agricultural and water infrastructure remain severely damaged, and displacement scenarios — including reported Israeli
consultations about transfers from Gaza — are being raised as active risks [593][598][516]. In sum, short-term prospects for
de-escalation are weak while the likelihood of intensification or wider regional spillover remains elevated unless a binding political
settlement and enforceable guarantees are reached promptly [506][531].
Displacement
Displacement Overview
Gaza
About 1.9 million people have been displaced in Gaza, with approximately 1.4 million living in overcrowded displacement sites
[703][637]. OCHA found rodents or pests frequently visible in over 80% of more than 1,600 sites assessed in mid-April 2026 [703].
Analysts and civil society describe a pattern of repeated, often forced internal displacement tied to shifting operational
'yellow'/'orange' lines, with some families displaced more than 15 times, and reports of proposals for population transfers abroad have
raised concerns about long-term population redistribution [656][683][699]. 
The Palestinian Ministry of Public Works and UNOPS signed a memorandum of understanding to support early recovery, transitional
shelter solutions and reconstruction planning to address displaced households' needs and supply-chain constraints for construction
materials [711].
West Bank
UN data indicate roughly 40,000 Palestinians were forcibly displaced from their homes in the West Bank since January 2025, while
OCHA documented 925 movement obstacles — the highest in 20 years — and nine Palestinian communities fully displaced in 2026;
demolitions and demolition notices (for example in Bireen) and rising detentions have continued to drive displacement risks
[653][697][708]. In the West Bank, accelerated settlement expansion, settler violence and displacement operations have driven
community displacement, with UN reporting noting plans for 1,080 new housing units and widespread killings and injuries in the 14
March–16 April period [715][653].
Living Conditions of the Displaced Population
Gaza
The scale of destruction in Gaza is severe: more than 371,000 housing units have been destroyed or damaged, over 60 percent of the
population remain homeless, and reconstruction needs are estimated at more than US$71 billion [637]. UN and partner field teams
found families sheltering in severely overcrowded, damaged buildings and tents with little to no sanitation, widespread rodents and
insects, and pronounced risks from untreated waste and collapsing services [640][663][703]. Nearly 90 percent of Gaza's water and
sanitation infrastructure has been destroyed or damaged, with UNICEF estimating average daily water availability at only about 7 litres
of drinking water and 16 litres of domestic water per person — well below emergency standards [705][690]. Médecins Sans Frontières
reported producing or distributing over 5.3 million litres of water per day in March 2026, meeting the minimum needs of roughly
407,000 people, while warning that distributions frequently run dry and access remains constrained [638].
Health services are critically degraded: only 296 of 683 health service points in Gaza were reported as functioning and just 23 were fully
operational, prompting WHO to call for the urgent entry of medicines and medical supplies [647][705]. WHO registered more than
17,000 rodent- and parasitic-related injuries since the start of 2026, and International Medical Corps reported that by 27 April 2026 its
mobile teams had covered 115 shelters and delivered 11,317 consultations — including vaccinations, referrals and chronic care — to
displaced populations [696][629][636].
13
The 2026 Global Report on Food Crises confirmed famine conditions in parts of Gaza and widespread acute food insecurity [670].
Humanitarian resupply has been severely constrained: agreed daily truck volumes have been met at only about 39 percent and fuel
deliveries are far below requirements, with aid operations repeatedly disrupted and humanitarian staff and drivers attacked or killed
during relief activities [626][648]. The prevalence of unexploded ordnance and explosive remnants continues to hinder safe returns
and recovery, with UN Mine Action Service reporting more than 1,200 people killed or injured by explosive ordnance since October 2023
[672].
Persons with disabilities, women (including female-headed households), and other vulnerable groups face disproportionate barriers
to shelter, services and rebuilding [637][713]. The Palestinian Ministry of Social Development reported some 64,616 children registered
as orphans in Gaza as of 2 May 2026, compounding protection and care needs among displaced populations [713].
Drivers of Displacement
Gaza
The primary driver of displacement in Gaza is the ongoing conflict, which has destroyed or damaged more than 371,000 housing units
and left over 60 percent of the population homeless [637]. Attacks on civilians and civilian infrastructure — including strikes that
damaged water, sanitation and health services and reproductive health facilities — have disrupted aid delivery and created
conditions that force people to flee and re-displace [660][696][716][686]. UN independent experts have warned that sexual and
gender-based violence is being used as a systematic tool of control contributing to forcible displacement across the occupied
Palestinian territory [631][677]. Extensive damage to agricultural land and ecological assets further undermines food security and
long-term livelihoods, prolonging displacement risks in Gaza and the wider occupied territory [639][661].
Access restrictions and the blockade — including limits on crossings, heavy equipment and debris removal — have hampered
reconstruction and return, increasing secondary displacement and risks for returnees [669][681][637]. Deep economic collapse and
mass joblessness have intensified vulnerability and the inability to rebuild livelihoods, undermining sustainable returns as reported by
UNCTAD and other UN agencies [669]. Humanitarian agencies warn that displaced populations face heightened public-health risks
from rodent-borne and water-borne diseases and insufficient WASH, shelter and medical services, which fuel repeated displacement
[702][696][660].
Integration
General
Unemployment and livelihood opportunities are severely constrained across the occupied Palestinian territory. PCBS data from 30 April
2026 recorded a 68% unemployment rate in Gaza — with 74% of 15–29 year-olds classified as NEET — a 28% unemployment rate in the
West Bank in Q4 2025, a loss of some 132,000 jobs, and a drop in Palestinian workers in Israel from 172,000 in Q3 2023 to 51,000 in Q4
2025 [654]. The economic base and productive infrastructure have been heavily damaged — around 70% of buildings and installations
including factories were reported damaged, and the economy contracted by approximately 84% — contributing to near-total
disruption of employment opportunities and local markets [706][641].
Formal education access has been disrupted by widespread damage to schools and closures, while alternative modalities such as the
Youm Jadeed audio programme reached over 160,000 children to support learning where classrooms are inaccessible [641][655].
Core social services remain severely degraded, reducing displaced people's access to healthcare, water and sanitation essential for
integration [705][647][660]. Repeated forced displacement alongside documented use of sexualized violence as a driver of coercive
displacement is eroding social cohesion and constraining safe movement and community reintegration [632][677].
The Palestinian government has implemented digital social protection measures and emergency cash modalities, including an
e-payment system and a digital registry reportedly covering some 350,000 families (around 90% coverage in Gaza), customs
exemptions for persons with disabilities, and orphan support initiatives [632][713]. Institutional recovery planning and partnerships
have been advanced — including an MoU to support housing, infrastructure, geospatial planning and supply-chain facilitation for
Gaza and the West Bank — but the scale of damage, access restrictions and resource gaps pose major barriers to durable integration
and long-term settlement [664][641].
Humanitarian Access
Humanitarian Access Overview
Gaza
Humanitarian access in the occupied Palestinian territory is highly constrained, with UN agencies and partners reporting severe limits
on aid flows and functioning services, particularly across Gaza where ongoing strikes and infrastructure damage hamper operations
[736][738]. Key humanitarian actors active on the ground include UN agencies (OCHA, WHO, UNRWA), International Medical Corps,
Médecins Sans Frontières (MSF), Save the Children and other INGOs delivering health, WASH, protection and emergency shelter
services [730][779][736][761]. Aid continues to be delivered by land convoys, crossings (including Rafah) and occasional air charters,
but these modalities face persistent security, bureaucratic and logistical restrictions [725][736][735]. Ceasefire-related humanitarian
protocols — including provisions for expanded truck throughput and Rafah operations — have not been fully implemented, and
UN-coordinated notification systems have only enabled limited assisted missions to hard-to-reach areas [735][738]. A humanitarian
protocol envisaged up to 600 trucks per day, but only about 39% of that number has entered and fuel deliveries are at roughly 14.9% of
required amounts [735].
14
Between 20 April and the following weekend, 104 patients were medically evacuated through the Rafah crossing [736]. Security risks —
including militarisation of space, evacuation orders, strikes on civilian infrastructure and attacks on aid workers — regularly disrupt
distributions and repair of essential services [779][739][738].
- Only 296 of 683 health service points in Gaza are functioning and just 23 are fully operational [736].
- UNMAS reports more than 1,200 people killed or injured by explosive ordnance in Gaza since October 2023, hindering safe access
and returns [738].
- MSF reported providing over 3.5 million litres of drinking water per day, meeting minimum needs for roughly 407,000 people, while
noting repeated rejections or non-responses to requests for critical WASH supplies and repair materials [750].
Barriers to Humanitarian Access
Gaza
Humanitarian access in Gaza is restricted by a convergence of infrastructure destruction, direct targeting of essential systems,
security risks, border and administrative denials of critical supplies, and political impediments linked to occupation and governance of
reconstruction processes [740][731][779]. Approximately 90% of Gaza's water and sanitation infrastructure has been damaged or
destroyed according to MSF and UN/EU/World Bank data, and 371,000+ housing units have been destroyed or damaged, with about 1.9
million people displaced — over 60% without homes [740][731][768]. MSF and UN assessments document that aid deliveries are
impeded by military displacement orders and attacks on water assets, and one-third of MSF requests for desalination units, pumps,
chlorine and other critical WASH items were rejected or left unanswered, with many approved consignments turned away at the
border [740][741][744]. In March 2026, MSF produced and distributed roughly 5.3 million litres of drinking water per day but reported
frequent disruptions due to access restrictions and attacks on water deliveries [744][741]. WHO and other UN actors have warned that
medicines, spare parts and bureaucratic facilitation are urgently needed to restore health services, but entry and administrative
restrictions continue to delay reconstruction of facilities [757][731]. Maritime and alternate delivery routes have also been blocked or
interrupted, including interception and detention of flotilla activists, preventing some independent deliveries to Gaza [769][795]. These
barriers disproportionately affect women, older people and persons with disabilities, amplifying the challenges of delivering timely,
dignified assistance [731][740].
Humanitarian Access Gaps
Gaza
Gaza's food system has effectively collapsed: Mercy Corps reported that 96% of agricultural land is destroyed or inaccessible and the
enclave's 2.1 million people remain heavily dependent on external food assistance, with famine conditions confirmed in parts of Gaza
and risks persisting into 2026 [809][763]. Bread production has fallen from about 300 to 200 tonnes per day, with WFP-supported
bakeries facing fuel and flour shortfalls; WFP warned it may reduce or halt bakery funding, risking sharp price rises [803]. Children,
pregnant and lactating women, older people and displaced families face acute unmet needs: 90% of children under two consume no
more than two food groups and some 41,000 children were at risk of death from malnutrition during the crisis period [809]. Some
adaptations provide short-term mitigation — community kitchens, emergency long-life rations, water trucking — but charity-run
kitchens and bakery schemes have been sharply reduced, and new initiatives such as Human Appeal's ready-to-eat meals
(announced 29 April 2026) aim to address the lack of cooking fuel and functional kitchens [803][805].
Humanitarian presence and operational capacity have contracted significantly: by end-2025 roughly 50 international organisations
were active, but 37 were told their Gaza registrations would expire and many NGOs faced new staff-registration requirements, while
convoys and deliveries are frequently delayed or blocked [803]. Access is repeatedly disrupted by security restrictions, evacuation
orders and restricted zones, with OCHA documenting a rise in movement constraints since October 2025 and MSF describing
suspensions or relocations of water distribution points that cut off vulnerable groups [743][779]. Health and sanitation shortfalls have
driven disease risk, with WHO's local representative reporting around 17,000 rodent- and ectoparasitic-infection cases so far this year
[820]. Recovery and reconstruction needs have been estimated at approximately USD 71.4 billion over the next decade [816]. Overall,
funding shortfalls, donor fatigue, supply denials, security risks and bureaucratic impediments remain the primary constraints
preventing scale-up and predictable delivery of lifesaving assistance [803][763][779].
Information, Communication and AAP
Information Landscape
Gaza
Humanitarian information on Gaza is produced by UN agencies (UNRWA, WHO), international NGOs (MSF, International Medical Corps),
local rights groups and media, but monitoring and reporting operate under severe access constraints and political contestation
[863][859][905][895]. International Medical Corps published operational figures as of 27 April, with mobile teams covering 115 shelters
with 31 units, delivering 11,317 consultations, providing acute medications to 6,119 people, chronic treatments to 1,332, referring 702
patients, administering routine vaccinations to 227 children, and distributing 760 hygiene kits [859]. UNRWA reported stockpiles and
distributions as of 28 April, including 1,099 mattresses, 4,796 pillows, 1,100 jerrycans and 290,000 aluminium foil sheets prepositioned in
decentralised warehouses, alongside distributions of thousands of hot and cold meals and bread packs through partners [863]. MSF
and WHO reporting documents widespread destruction of water and sanitation systems — WHO noting some 111,500 cases of disease
or infestation linked to external parasites, and MSF reporting that roughly one-third of its requests for critical water supplies since
October 2023 were rejected or left unanswered [850][905][843].
15
Israeli access and regulatory measures have tightened information and operational flows: a new requirement to share NGO staff
names led to many international organisations being warned their Gaza registrations would expire, and COGAT-mapped coordination
zones (the Orange/Yellow lines) limit movement and create uncertainty for aid delivery and civilian awareness of safe areas
[906][886]. Independent monitoring and casualty recording are likely incomplete — Action on Armed Violence estimates
English-language media captured only about one-third of deaths in high-intensity conflicts such as Gaza, indicating significant
underreporting and data gaps [889]. The April interception of multiple Gaza-bound flotillas further underscores contested narratives
and verification challenges, as organisers, several governments and Israeli authorities offered conflicting accounts of events and
detainee treatment, complicating corroboration and diplomatic channels for aid access [911][920][917]. Assessments and reports also
describe reliance on water trucking, crowding and safety risks at distribution points, and disproportionate barriers for children, older
people, persons with disabilities and women in reaching services and information [843][905][886].
Communication Channels
Gaza
Humanitarian actors continue face-to-face outreach through mobile primary satellite units: as of 27 April, International Medical Corps'
31 units had covered 115 shelters and delivered 11,317 consultations and related services to displaced populations [859]. Radio and
audio programming remain important complementary channels — the Youm Jadeed audio drama (Lapis/Education Above All
partners, supported by the International Rescue Committee) reached over 160,000 children in disrupted settings, using radio, simple
audio files and group listening to deliver information and psychosocial support [861].
Digital access has been severely constrained by prolonged outages, with the UN reporting an internet shutdown lasting 61 days that
cut people off from vital information and independent voices [855]. Movement restrictions and shifting military control have further
disrupted in-person and institutional channels: Israeli evacuation orders and area control left large parts of Gaza inaccessible to
civilians and humanitarians, and as of March 2026 Israeli forces controlled and forbade access to about 58% of the Strip, undermining
routine humanitarian communication and service delivery [843]. The WASH Cluster assessed 43% of WASH facilities as inaccessible to
the population and humanitarians, with an additional 10% accessible only with permission [843]. Coordination requirements and new
maps issued by Israeli authorities — including the "orange" area adjacent to the Yellow Line — obliged aid groups to coordinate with
the military, narrowed safe operating space, and sowed confusion among displaced people about where assistance could reach
them; updated maps were shared with aid groups in mid-March, and together the orange and yellow zones cordon nearly two-thirds
of Gaza [886][864]. Maritime interdictions of aid efforts and reported jamming of flotilla communications further demonstrated
constraints on humanitarian coordination and the ability of civil society actors to communicate during relief attempts [926][928].
Barriers to Information
Gaza
Attacks on media workers, internet shutdowns and targeted news blackouts have severely constrained civilians' access to reliable
information in Gaza and the occupied Palestinian territory [880][903]. According to the United Nations, more than 260 media
professionals had been killed by December 2025, contributing to a climate of fear and increased self-censorship among journalists
covering the conflict [903]. UN reporting and press-freedom monitoring note repeated restrictions on access for independent
reporters and growing efforts to control or silence media, undermining transparent reporting and public accountability [880][903].
Operational denials and movement restrictions further impede two-way communication with affected populations. OCHA reported
that strikes and the killing of aid-contracted drivers have suspended operations at key water points, and that displacement orders
issued on 29 April further curtailed humanitarian access and the ability to deliver information and services to displaced communities
[855]. Nearly 90% of Gaza's water and sanitation infrastructure has been destroyed, with MSF documenting that one-third of requests
to import critical water and sanitation supplies were rejected or left unanswered, limiting both outreach and accountability to affected
people [875].
Misinformation and Rumors
Gaza
Reporting around late April–early May 2026 documents a near-total collapse of Gaza's media environment and an "information
vacuum" that severely limits independent reporting and documentation of violations [898][958]. The Palestinian Journalists' Union and
other sources report at least 262 Palestinian journalists and media professionals killed since the start of the war, alongside large
numbers of arrests and disappearances — more than 240 journalists arrested, with over 40 still in detention — which collectively
undermine accountability, on-the-ground verification, and create expanded space for misinformation and polarization
[958][898][954].
Analyses point to organised information campaigns and heavy investment in narrative management (so-called "Hasbara"), as well as
the growing role of AI and digitally altered content in spreading misleading material and shaping public perceptions [890][903]. UN
agencies, UNESCO and press-freedom advocates have called for independent investigations, protection of journalists, and restored
information flows as essential measures to counter disinformation and restore public trust — actions highlighted around World Press
Freedom Day, 3 May 2026 [898][955][958].
Accountability Needs and Transparency
Gaza
Humanitarian access and accountability in Gaza are severely undermined by restrictions, site-level control and attacks that have
harmed and killed aid workers and suspended operations, impeding affected people's ability to give feedback or seek redress
[843][827].
16
WHO had verified 149 attacks on healthcare (100 deaths, 233 injuries) as of 27 April, disrupting reporting, complaint handling and
service continuity [859]. Media collapse and direct targeting of journalists have further eroded independent reporting and public
scrutiny, with some outlets destroyed and roughly 300 crimes and violations against journalists recorded since the start of 2026,
limiting affected communities' access to information and platforms to voice concerns [898][964]. Independent monitoring and
casualty recording are known to be substantially underreported, prompting calls to collect, disaggregate and publicly share data on
explosive weapons use and civilian harm to support accountability and victim assistance [889].
Aid delivery figures are also contested: the ceasefire protocol envisaged 600 trucks per day but only about 39% of that number
actually entered, while fuel deliveries amounted to roughly 14.9% of required quantities, constraining service restoration and
transparent aid distribution [839]. The Board of Peace asserts markedly increased aid delivery via the CMCC, while frontline aid
workers report dwindling funds, donor fatigue and Israeli restrictions that reduce the actual aid reaching people, indicating low
community trust in official claims and gaps in transparent delivery [831].
Nascent transparency and accountability measures are being developed: the Palestinian Ministry of Public Works and Housing and
UNOPS signed a five-year MoU to support reconstruction with supply-chain and material management measures and a cloud-based
information management system to quantify damage and prioritise interventions, which could strengthen public disclosure and
participatory planning if implemented as envisaged [828][836]. UN experts and rights actors stress that reconstruction must be
inclusive, participatory, transparent and accountable, with Palestinians central to decision-making, to avoid reproducing structural
discrimination and to restore trust in response processes [901].
Legal and Policy
Legal and Policy Framework
Gaza
The legal framework governing the crisis is anchored in International Humanitarian Law, the Geneva Conventions, and International
Court of Justice findings that identified a plausible risk of genocide and ordered measures to prevent it and ensure unhindered
humanitarian assistance to Gaza [971][1047]. Regional and international actors — including the EU, ASEAN, and UN bodies — have
called for full implementation of the Gaza Peace Plan and UNSC Resolution 2803 (2025), which endorses the establishment of an
International Stabilization Force (ISF) and demands unhindered humanitarian access in accordance with IHL and human rights law
[1018][1019]. However, ISF-related mechanisms have seen limited commitments: only a handful of countries pledged troops, the United
States planned to reduce its troop presence, and the Civil-Military Coordination Centre (CMCC) was reported to close or transition on 1
May 2026, constraining monitoring and enforcement of the ceasefire [1004][990].
At the domestic level, the Israeli Knesset adopted a law on 30 March 2026 authorizing the death penalty within the military court
system applicable to Palestinians — a measure that UN treaty bodies, including the UN Committee on the Elimination of Racial
Discrimination, and human rights organizations described as discriminatory and urged to be repealed as incompatible with fair-trial
and non-discrimination obligations under international law [1047][1035][1013][1011]. Parallel legislative and administrative
developments include accelerated settlement approvals and state-funded settlement expansion — including the approval of 34 new
settlement developments in April 2026 and related infrastructure funding — actions that international legal commentators and UN
